It is the main interface for external components\\n44:  * to make changes to the state of the cluster. For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\\n45:  * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\\n46:  * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\\n47:  * of the workers.\\n48:  * </p>\\n49:  * <p>\\n50:  * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\\n51:  * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). The non-Java interfaces to the cluster (REST API and CLI) are very simple\\n52:  * wrappers of the functionality provided by this interface.\\n53:  * </p>\\n54:  * <p>\\n55:  * In standalone mode, the implementation of this interface will be trivial because no coordination is needed. In that case,\\n56:  * the implementation will mainly be delegating tasks directly to other components. For example, when creating a new\\n57:  * connector in standalone mode, there is no need to persist the config and the connector and its tasks must run in the\\n58:  * same process, so the standalone herder implementation can immediately instantiate and start the connector and its\\n59:  * tasks.\\n60:  * </p>\\n61:  */\\n62: public interface Herder {\\n63: \\n64:     void start();\\n65: \\n66:     void stop();\\n67: \\n68:     /**\\n69:      * @return whether the worker is ready; i.e., it has completed all initialization and startup\\n70:      * steps such as creating internal topics, joining a cluster, etc.\\n71:      */\\n72:     boolean isReady();\\n73: \\n74:     /**\\n75:      * Check for worker health; i.e., its ability to service external requests from the user such\\n76:      * as creating, reconfiguring, and deleting connectors\\n77:      * @param callback callback to invoke once worker health is assured\\n78:      */\\n79:     void healthCheck(Callback<Void> callback);\\n80: \\n81:     /**\\n82:      * Get a list of connectors currently running in this cluster. For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\\n45:  * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\\n46:  * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\\n47:  * of the workers.\\n48:  * </p>\\n49:  * <p>\\n50:  * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\\n51:  * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). In that case,\\n56:  * the implementation will mainly be delegating tasks directly to other components. It is the main interface for external components\n44:  * to make changes to the state of the cluster. For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\n45:  * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\n46:  * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\n47:  * of the workers.\n48:  * </p>\n49:  * <p>\n50:  * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\n51:  * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). The non-Java interfaces to the cluster (REST API and CLI) are very simple\n52:  * wrappers of the functionality provided by this interface.\n53:  * </p>\n54:  * <p>\n55:  * In standalone mode, the implementation of this interface will be trivial because no coordination is needed. In that case,\n56:  * the implementation will mainly be delegating tasks directly to other components. For example, when creating a new\n57:  * connector in standalone mode, there is no need to persist the config and the connector and its tasks must run in the\n58:  * same process, so the standalone herder implementation can immediately instantiate and start the connector and its\n59:  * tasks.\n60:  * </p>\n61:  */\n62: public interface Herder {\n63: \n64:     void start();\n65: \n66:     void stop();\n67: \n68:     /**\n69:      * @return whether the worker is ready; i.e., it has completed all initialization and startup\n70:      * steps such as creating internal topics, joining a cluster, etc.\n71:      */\n72:     boolean isReady();\n73: \n74:     /**\n75:      * Check for worker health; i.e., its ability to service external requests from the user such\n76:      * as creating, reconfiguring, and deleting connectors\n77:      * @param callback callback to invoke once worker health is assured\n78:      */\n79:     void healthCheck(Callback<Void> callback);\n80: \n81:     /**\n82:      * Get a list of connectors currently running in this cluster. org.apache.kafka.connect.runtime.ConnectorConfig.VALUE_CONVERTER_VERSION_CONFIG;\\n106: \\n107: \\n108: /**\\n109:  * Abstract Herder implementation which handles connector/task lifecycle tracking. Extensions\\n110:  * must invoke the lifecycle hooks appropriately.\\n111:  * <p>\\n112:  * This class takes the following approach for sending status updates to the backing store:\\n113:  *\\n114:  * <ol>\\n115:  * <li>\\n116:  *    When the connector or task is starting, we overwrite the previous state blindly. This ensures that\\n117:  *    every rebalance will reset the state of tasks to the proper state. The intuition is that there should\\n118:  *    be less chance of write conflicts when the worker has just received its assignment and is starting tasks.\\n119:  *    In particular, this prevents us from depending on the generation absolutely. If the group disappears\\n120:  *    and the generation is reset, then we'll overwrite the status information with the older (and larger)\\n121:  *    generation with the updated one. The danger of this approach is that slow starting tasks may cause the\\n122:  *    status to be overwritten after a rebalance has completed.\\n123:  *\\n124:  * <li>\\n125:  *    If the connector or task fails or is shutdown, we use {@link StatusBackingStore#putSafe(ConnectorStatus)},\\n126:  *    which provides a little more protection if the worker is no longer in the group (in which case the\\n127:  *    task may have already been started on another worker). Obviously this is still racy. If the task has just\\n128:  *    started on another worker, we may not have the updated status cached yet. In this case, we'll overwrite\\n129:  *    the value which will cause the state to be inconsistent (most likely until the next rebalance). Until\\n130:  *    we have proper producer groups with fenced groups, there is not much else we can do.\\n131:  * </ol>\\n132:  */\\n133: public abstract class AbstractHerder implements Herder, ConfigBackingStore configBackingStore;\\n142:     private volatile boolean ready = false;\\n143:     private final Plugin<ConnectorClientConfigOverridePolicy> connectorClientConfigOverridePolicyPlugin;\\n144:     private final ExecutorService connectorExecutor;\\n145:     private final Time time;\\n146:     protected final Loggers loggers;\\n147: \\n148:     private final CachedConnectors cachedConnectors;\\n149: \\n150:     public AbstractHerder(Worker worker,\\n151:                           String workerId,\\n152:                           String kafkaClusterId,\\n153:                           StatusBackingStore statusBackingStore,\\n154:                           ConfigBackingStore configBackingStore,\\n155:                           ConnectorClientConfigOverridePolicy connectorClientConfigOverridePolicy,\\n156:                           Time time) {\\n157:         this.worker = worker;\\n158:         this.worker.herder = this;\\n159:         this.workerId = workerId;\\n160:         this.kafkaClusterId = kafkaClusterId;\\n161:         this.statusBackingStore = statusBackingStore;\\n162:         this.configBackingStore = configBackingStore;\\n163:         this.connectorClientConfigOverridePolicyPlugin = Plugin.wrapInstance(\\n164:                 connectorClientConfigOverridePolicy,\\n165:                 worker.metrics().metrics(),\\n166:                 WorkerConfig.CONNECTOR_CLIENT_POLICY_CLASS_CONFIG);\\n167:         this.connectorExecutor = Executors.newCachedThreadPool();\\n168:         this.time Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle pattern across its broker, controller, and Connect framework components.\\n\\n## Core Lifecycle Interface\\n\\nThe foundation of the service lifecycle pattern is defined in **kafka/server/Server.scala**, which provides the Server trait with three core methods:\\n- startup(): Unit - Initiates server startup\\n- shutdown(): Unit - Initiates server shutdown  \\n- awaitShutdown(): Unit - Waits for shutdown completion\\n\\nThe trait also defines ProcessStatus states: SHUTDOWN, STARTING, STARTED, and SHUTTING_DOWN, which track the lifecycle state machine.\\n\\n## Service Startup and Shutdown Ordering\\n\\n**KafkaRaftServer (kafka/server/KafkaRaftServer.scala)** orchestrates the dependent service startup and shutdown:\\n- **Startup order (lines 90-98)**: Controller starts first, then broker\\n  ```\\n  controller.foreach(_.startup())\\n  broker.foreach(_.startup())\\n  ```\\n- **Shutdown order (lines 100-107)**: Broker stops first (reverse order), then controller\\n  ```\\n  broker.foreach(_.shutdown())\\n  controller.foreach(_.shutdown())\\n  ```\\n\\nThis reverse-order shutdown ensures dependent services stop in the correct sequence. The shutdown happens in this order because the controller may be needed for controlled shutdown of the broker.\\n\\n## Broker Lifecycle Implementation\\n\\n**BrokerServer (kafka/server/BrokerServer.scala)** provides an extensive startup sequence (lines 189-607) that initializes components in dependency order:\\n1. Shared server startup\\n2. Scheduler initialization\\n3. Log manager and metadata cache creation\\n4. Lifecycle manager creation\\n5. Socket server and request handlers\\n6. Replica manager\\n7. Coordinators (group, transaction, share)\\n8. Various managers and publishers\\n\\nThe shutdown method (lines 729-843) carefully dismantles services in reverse order using CoreUtils.swallow() for fault-tolerant shutdown, ensuring one failure doesn't prevent other services from shutting down.\\n\\n## Shared Infrastructure Coordination\\n\\n**SharedServer (kafka/server/SharedServer.scala)** manages components shared between broker and controller with reference counting:\\n- startForBroker() and startForController() methods handle conditional startup\\n- Proper synchronization ensures single initialization despite multiple callers\\n- stopForBroker() and stopForController() enable coordinated shutdown\\n\\n## Broker State Management\\n\\n**BrokerLifecycleManager (kafka/server/BrokerLifecycleManager.scala)** manages the broker's lifecycle state with:\\n- Event queue paradigm for sequential state transitions\\n- Future-based coordination for startup/shutdown milestones\\n- Registration and heartbeat management with controller\\n- Methods: beginControlledShutdown(), beginShutdown() for graceful transitions\\n\\n## Connect Framework Lifecycle Pattern\\n\\nThe Kafka Connect framework implements parallel lifecycle patterns:\\n\\n**Herder interface (org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/Herder.java)** defines start() and stop() methods for the main coordinator of worker and connector lifecycle.\\n\\n**StatusBackingStore interface (org/apache/kafka/connect/storage/StatusBackingStore.java)** provides start() and stop() for dependent services managing connector/task status.\\n\\n**AbstractHerder (org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/AbstractHerder.java)** implements lifecycle coordination:\\n- startServices() (lines 180-184): Starts worker, status backing store, config backing store in order\\n- stopServices() (lines 186-192): Stops in reverse order with cleanup:\\n  1. statusBackingStore.stop()\\n  2. configBackingStore.stop()\\n  3. worker.stop()\\n  4. connectorExecutor.shutdown()\\n  5. connectorClientConfigOverridePolicyPlugin close\\n\\n**StandaloneHerder (org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/standalone/StandaloneHerder.java)** implements the concrete lifecycle:\\n- start() (lines 110-114): Calls startServices() to initialize all dependencies\\n- stop() (lines 117-130): Cleanly stops all connectors, then calls stopServices() in reverse order\\n\\n## Key Lifecycle Patterns\\n\\n1. **Dependent Service Ordering**: Services with dependencies start after their dependencies and stop before them\\n2. **Status State Machine**: Explicit state transitions prevent invalid operations and enable monitoring\\n3. **Reverse-Order Shutdown**: Shutdown sequence reverses startup sequence to maintain invariants\\n4. **Fault-Tolerant Cleanup**: CoreUtils.swallow() ensures one failure doesn't prevent other shutdown tasks\\n5. **Future-Based Coordination**: CompletableFuture enables waiting for asynchronous startup/shutdown milestones\\n6. Service Startup and Shutdown Ordering\\n\\n**KafkaRaftServer (kafka/server/KafkaRaftServer.scala)** orchestrates the dependent service startup and shutdown:\\n- **Startup order (lines 90-98)**: then controller\\n  ```\\n  broker.foreach(_.shutdown())\\n  controller.foreach(_.shutdown())\\n  ```\\n\\nThis reverse-order shutdown ensures dependent services stop in the correct sequence. The shutdown happens in this order because the controller may be needed for controlled shutdown of the broker.\\n\\n## Broker Lifecycle Implementation\\n\\n**BrokerServer (kafka/server/BrokerServer.scala)** provides an extensive startup sequence (lines 189-607) that initializes components in dependency order:\\n1. callers\\n- stopForBroker() and stopForController() enable coordinated shutdown\\n\\n## Broker State Management\\n\\n**BrokerLifecycleManager (kafka/server/BrokerLifecycleManager.scala)** manages the controller\\n- Methods: beginControlledShutdown(), beginShutdown() for graceful transitions\\n\\n## Connect Framework Lifecycle Pattern\\n\\nThe Kafka Connect framework implements parallel lifecycle patterns:\\n\\n**Herder interface (org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/Herder.java)** defines start() and stop() methods for the main coordinator of worker and connector order\\n\\n## Key Lifecycle Patterns\\n\\n1. **Dependent Service Ordering**: Services with dependencies start after their dependencies and stop before them\\n2. **Status State Machine**: Explicit state transitions prevent invalid operations and enable monitoring\\n3. **Reverse-Order Shutdown**: Shutdown sequence reverses startup sequence to maintain invariants\\n4. **Fault-Tolerant Cleanup**:
